           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Exploring-Eye-Movements-in-Patients-with-Glaucoma-When-Viewing-a-Driving-Scene-pone.0009710.s001.ogv" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Scientists track eye movements in glaucoma patients to check vision impairment while driving
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       It is important that people be examined by someone specializing in low vision care prior to other rehabilitation training to rule out potential medical or surgical correction for the problem and to establish a careful baseline refraction and prescription of both normal and low vision glasses and optical aids. Only a doctor is qualified to evaluate visual functioning of a compromised visual system effectively.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/American_Medical_Association" title="American Medical Association">
        American Medical Association
       </a>
       provides an approach to evaluating visual loss as it affects an individual's ability to perform activities of daily living.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-colenbrander_21-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-colenbrander-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Screening adults who have no symptoms is of uncertain benefit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USPSTF2016_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-USPSTF2016-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">
        Prevention
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Prevention">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The World Health Organization estimates that 80% of visual loss is either preventable or curable with treatment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This includes cataracts, onchocerciasis, trachoma, glaucoma, diabetic retinopathy, uncorrected refractive errors, and some cases of childhood blindness.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2015Cause_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-WHO2015Cause-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Center for Disease Control and Prevention estimates that half of blindness in the United States is preventable.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2011_2-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-CDC2011-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">
        Treatment
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Treatment">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tommy_Edison_blind_cooking.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1600" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Tommy_Edison_blind_cooking.jpg/220px-Tommy_Edison_blind_cooking.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Tommy_Edison_blind_cooking.jpg/330px-Tommy_Edison_blind_cooking.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Tommy_Edison_blind_cooking.jpg/440px-Tommy_Edison_blind_cooking.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tommy_Edison_blind_cooking.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Tommy Edison, a blind film critic, demonstrates for his viewers how a blind person can cook alone.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Aside from medical help, various sources provide information, rehabilitation, education, and work and social integration.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mobility">
        Mobility
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mobility">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Long_cane_folded.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2288" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Long_cane_folded.jpg/220px-Long_cane_folded.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Long_cane_folded.jpg/330px-Long_cane_folded.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Long_cane_folded.jpg/440px-Long_cane_folded.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Long_cane_folded.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Folded long cane
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caoguia2006.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1287" data-file-width="1851" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Caoguia2006.jpg/220px-Caoguia2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Caoguia2006.jpg/330px-Caoguia2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Caoguia2006.jpg/440px-Caoguia2006.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caoguia2006.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A blind man is assisted by a
         <a href="/wiki/Guide_dog" title="Guide dog">
          guide dog
         </a>
         in Brasília, Brazil
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blind_girl_feels_shape_of_vehicle_near_Mana_village,_Uttarakhand.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1440" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Blind_girl_feels_shape_of_vehicle_near_Mana_village%2C_Uttarakhand.jpg/220px-Blind_girl_feels_shape_of_vehicle_near_Mana_village%2C_Uttarakhand.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Blind_girl_feels_shape_of_vehicle_near_Mana_village%2C_Uttarakhand.jpg/330px-Blind_girl_feels_shape_of_vehicle_near_Mana_village%2C_Uttarakhand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Blind_girl_feels_shape_of_vehicle_near_Mana_village%2C_Uttarakhand.jpg/440px-Blind_girl_feels_shape_of_vehicle_near_Mana_village%2C_Uttarakhand.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blind_girl_feels_shape_of_vehicle_near_Mana_village,_Uttarakhand.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Blind girl feels shape of vehicle near
         <a href="/wiki/Mana,_India" title="Mana, India">
          Mana village, Uttarakhand
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Visually_impaired_girl_negotiating_a_rock_while_rock_climbing.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2304" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Visually_impaired_girl_negotiating_a_rock_while_rock_climbing.jpg/170px-Visually_impaired_girl_negotiating_a_rock_while_rock_climbing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Visually_impaired_girl_negotiating_a_rock_while_rock_climbing.jpg/255px-Visually_impaired_girl_negotiating_a_rock_while_rock_climbing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Visually_impaired_girl_negotiating_a_rock_while_rock_climbing.jpg/340px-Visually_impaired_girl_negotiating_a_rock_while_rock_climbing.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Visually_impaired_girl_negotiating_a_rock_while_rock_climbing.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Visually impaired girl negotiating a rock while rock climbing
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Many people with serious visual impairments can travel independently, using a wide range of tools and techniques. Orientation and mobility specialists are professionals who are specifically trained to teach people with visual impairments how to travel safely, confidently, and independently in the home and the community. These professionals can also help blind people to practice travelling on specific routes which they may use often, such as the route from one's house to a convenience store. Becoming familiar with an environment or route can make it much easier for a blind person to navigate successfully.
      </p>
      <p>
       Tools such as the
       <a href="/wiki/White_cane" title="White cane">
        white cane
       </a>
       with a red tip – the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_symbols" title="International symbols">
        international symbol
       </a>
       of blindness – may also be used to improve mobility. A long cane is used to extend the user's range of touch sensation. It is usually swung in a low sweeping motion, across the intended path of travel, to detect obstacles. However, techniques for cane travel can vary depending on the user and/or the situation. Some visually impaired persons do not carry these kinds of canes, opting instead for the shorter, lighter identification (ID) cane. Still others require a support cane. The choice depends on the individual's vision, motivation, and other factors.
      </p>
      <p>
       A small number of people employ
       <a href="/wiki/Guide_dog" title="Guide dog">
        guide dogs
       </a>
       to assist in mobility. These dogs are trained to navigate around various obstacles, and to indicate when it becomes necessary to go up or down a step. However, the helpfulness of guide dogs is limited by the inability of dogs to understand complex directions. The human half of the guide dog team does the directing, based upon skills acquired through previous mobility training. In this sense, the handler might be likened to an aircraft's navigator, who must know how to get from one place to another, and the dog to the pilot, who gets them there safely.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/GPS_for_the_visually_impaired" title="GPS for the visually impaired">
        GPS devices
       </a>
       can also be used as a mobility aid. Such software can assist blind people with orientation and navigation, but it is not a replacement for traditional mobility tools such as white canes and guide dogs.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some blind people are skilled at
       <a href="/wiki/Human_echolocation" title="Human echolocation">
        echolocating
       </a>
       silent objects simply by producing mouth clicks and listening to the returning echoes. It has been shown that blind echolocation experts use what is normally the "visual" part of their brain to process the echoes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thaler2011_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Thaler2011-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reader_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Reader-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Government actions are sometimes taken to make public places more accessible to blind people. Public transportation is freely available to the blind in many cities.
       <a href="/wiki/Tactile_paving" title="Tactile paving">
        Tactile paving
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pedestrian_crossing#Enhancements_for_disabled_people" title="Pedestrian crossing">
        audible traffic signals
       </a>
       can make it easier and safer for visually impaired pedestrians to cross streets. In addition to making rules about who can and cannot use a cane, some governments mandate the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Priority_(right_of_way)" title="Priority (right of way)">
        right-of-way
       </a>
       be given to users of white canes or guide dogs.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Reading_and_magnification">
        Reading and magnification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Reading and magnification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Watch_for_the_blind2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1041" data-file-width="757" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Watch_for_the_blind2.jpg/170px-Watch_for_the_blind2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Watch_for_the_blind2.jpg/255px-Watch_for_the_blind2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Watch_for_the_blind2.jpg/340px-Watch_for_the_blind2.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Watch_for_the_blind2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Braille_watch" title="Braille watch">
          Braille watch
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Most visually impaired people who are not totally blind read print, either of a regular size or enlarged by magnification devices. Many also read
       <a href="/wiki/Large-print" title="Large-print">
        large-print
       </a>
       , which is easier for them to read without such devices. A variety of
       <a href="/wiki/Magnifying_glass" title="Magnifying glass">
        magnifying glasses
       </a>
       , some handheld, and some on desktops, can make reading easier for them.
      </p>
      <p>
       Others read
       <a href="/wiki/Braille" title="Braille">
        Braille
       </a>
       (or the infrequently used
       <a href="/wiki/Moon_type" title="Moon type">
        Moon type
       </a>
       ), or rely on
       <a href="/wiki/Audiobook" title="Audiobook">
        talking books
       </a>
       and readers or
       <a href="/wiki/Reading_machine" title="Reading machine">
        reading machines
       </a>
       , which convert printed text to speech or
       <a href="/wiki/Braille" title="Braille">
        Braille
       </a>
       . They use computers with special hardware such as
       <a href="/wiki/Image_scanner" title="Image scanner">
        scanners
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refreshable_Braille_display" title="Refreshable Braille display">
        refreshable Braille displays
       </a>
       as well as software written specifically for the blind, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Optical_character_recognition" title="Optical character recognition">
        optical character recognition
       </a>
       applications and
       <a href="/wiki/Screen_reader" title="Screen reader">
        screen readers
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Some people access these materials through agencies for the blind, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Library_Service_for_the_Blind_and_Physically_Handicapped" title="National Library Service for the Blind and Physically Handicapped">
        National Library Service for the Blind and Physically Handicapped
       </a>
       in the United States, the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Library_for_the_Blind" title="National Library for the Blind">
        National Library for the Blind
       </a>
       or the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_National_Institute_for_the_Blind" title="Royal National Institute for the Blind">
        RNIB
       </a>
       in the United Kingdom.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Closed-circuit_television" title="Closed-circuit television">
        Closed-circuit televisions
       </a>
       , equipment that enlarges and contrasts textual items, are a more
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-tech" title="High-tech">
        high-tech
       </a>
       alternative to traditional magnification devices.
      </p>
      <p>
       There are also over 100
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_reading_service" title="Radio reading service">
        radio reading services
       </a>
       throughout the world that provide people with vision impairments with readings from periodicals over the radio. The International Association of Audio Information Services provides links to all of these organizations.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Computers">
        Computers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Computers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Access technology such as
       <a href="/wiki/Screen_reader" title="Screen reader">
        screen readers
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Screen_magnifier" title="Screen magnifier">
        screen magnifiers
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refreshable_Braille_display" title="Refreshable Braille display">
        refreshable Braille displays
       </a>
       enable the blind to use mainstream computer applications and
       <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">
        mobile phones
       </a>
       . The availability of assistive technology is increasing, accompanied by concerted efforts to ensure the accessibility of information technology to all potential users, including the blind. Later versions of
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">
        Microsoft Windows
       </a>
       include an Accessibility Wizard &amp; Magnifier for those with partial vision, and
       <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Narrator" title="Microsoft Narrator">
        Microsoft Narrator
       </a>
       , a simple screen reader.
       <a href="/wiki/Linux_distribution" title="Linux distribution">
        Linux distributions
       </a>
       (as
       <a href="/wiki/Live_CD" title="Live CD">
        live CDs
       </a>
       ) for the blind include
       <a href="/wiki/Oralux" title="Oralux">
        Oralux
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adriane_Knoppix" title="Adriane Knoppix">
        Adriane Knoppix
       </a>
       , the latter developed in part by
       <a href="/wiki/Klaus_Knopper" title="Klaus Knopper">
        Adriane Knopper
       </a>
       who has a visual impairment. Mac OS also comes with a built-in screen reader, called
       <a href="/wiki/VoiceOver" title="VoiceOver">
        VoiceOver
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The movement towards greater
       <a href="/wiki/Web_accessibility" title="Web accessibility">
        web accessibility
       </a>
       is opening a far wider number of websites to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adaptive_technology" title="Adaptive technology">
        adaptive technology
       </a>
       , making the web a more inviting place for visually impaired surfers.
      </p>
      <p>
       Experimental approaches in
       <a href="/wiki/Sensory_substitution" title="Sensory substitution">
        sensory substitution
       </a>
       are beginning to provide access to arbitrary live views from a
       <a href="/wiki/Camera" title="Camera">
        camera
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Modified visual output that includes large print and/or clear simple graphics can be of benefit to users with some residual vision.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dynamicdiversity_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-dynamicdiversity-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_aids_and_techniques">
        Other aids and techniques
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other aids and techniques">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Banknote_feature.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="277" data-file-width="360" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/df/Banknote_feature.JPG/220px-Banknote_feature.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/df/Banknote_feature.JPG/330px-Banknote_feature.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/df/Banknote_feature.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Banknote_feature.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A
         <a href="/wiki/Canadian_currency_tactile_feature" title="Canadian currency tactile feature">
          tactile feature
         </a>
         on a
         <a href="/wiki/Banknotes_of_the_Canadian_dollar" title="Banknotes of the Canadian dollar">
          Canadian banknote
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Blind people may use talking equipment such as
       <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">
        thermometers
       </a>
       , watches, clocks,
       <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale" title="Weighing scale">
        scales
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Calculator" title="Calculator">
        calculators
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">
        compasses
       </a>
       . They may also enlarge or mark dials on devices such as ovens and thermostats to make them usable. Other techniques used by blind people to assist them in daily activities include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Adaptations of
        <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">
         coins
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">
         banknotes
        </a>
        so that the value can be determined by touch. For example:
        <ul>
         <li>
          In some currencies, such as the
          <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">
           euro
          </a>
          , the
          <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">
           pound sterling
          </a>
          and the
          <a href="/wiki/Indian_rupee" title="Indian rupee">
           Indian rupee
          </a>
          , the size of a note increases with its value.
         </li>
         <li>
          On US coins, pennies and dimes, and nickels and quarters are similar in size. The larger denominations (dimes and quarters) have ridges along the sides (historically used to prevent the "shaving" of precious metals from the coins), which can now be used for identification.
         </li>
         <li>
          Some currencies'
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banknotes" title="Banknotes">
           banknotes
          </a>
          have a tactile feature to indicate denomination. For example, the
          <a href="/wiki/Canadian_currency_tactile_feature" title="Canadian currency tactile feature">
           Canadian currency tactile feature
          </a>
          is a system of raised dots in one corner, based on Braille cells but not standard
          <a href="/wiki/Braille" title="Braille">
           Braille
          </a>
          .
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
           <a href="#cite_note-51">
            [51]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
         <li>
          It is also possible to fold notes in different ways to assist recognition.
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        Labeling and tagging clothing and other personal items
       </li>
       <li>
        Placing different types of food at different positions on a dinner plate
       </li>
       <li>
        Marking controls of household appliances
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Most people, once they have been visually impaired for long enough, devise their own adaptive strategies in all areas of personal and professional management.
      </p>
      <p>
       For the blind, there are books in braille, audio-books, and text-to-speech computer programs, machines and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-book_readers" title="E-book readers">
        e-book readers
       </a>
       . Low vision people can make use of these tools as well as
       <a href="/wiki/Large-print" title="Large-print">
        large-print
       </a>
       reading materials and e-book readers that provide large
       <a href="/wiki/Font" title="Font">
        font
       </a>
       sizes.
      </p>
      <p>
       Computers are important tools of integration for the visually impaired person. They allow, using standard or specific programs, screen magnification and conversion of text into sound or touch (Braille line), and are useful for all levels of visual handicap.
       <a href="/wiki/Optical_character_recognition" title="Optical character recognition">
        OCR
       </a>
       scanners can, in conjunction with text-to-speech software, read the contents of books and documents aloud via computer. Vendors also build closed-circuit televisions that electronically magnify paper, and even change its contrast and color, for visually impaired users. For more information, consult
       <a href="/wiki/Assistive_technology" title="Assistive technology">
        Assistive technology
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In adults with low vision there is no conclusive evidence supporting one form of reading aid over another.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Virgili_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Virgili-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In several studies stand-based closed-circuit television and hand-held closed-circuit television allowed faster reading than optical aids.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Virgili_52-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Virgili-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While electronic aids may allow faster reading for individuals with low vision, portability, ease of use, and affordability must be considered for people.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Virgili_52-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Virgili-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Children with low vision sometimes have reading delays, but do benefit from phonics-based beginning reading instruction methods. Engaging phonics instruction is multisensory, highly motivating, and hands-on. Typically students are first taught the most frequent sounds of the alphabet letters, especially the so-called short vowel sounds, then taught to blend sounds together with three-letter consonant-vowel-consonant words such as cat, red, sit, hot, sun. Hands-on (or kinesthetically appealing) VERY enlarged print materials such as those found in "The Big Collection of Phonics Flipbooks" by Lynn Gordon (Scholastic, 2010) are helpful for teaching word families and blending skills to beginning readers with low vision. Beginning reading instructional materials should focus primarily on the lower-case letters, not the capital letters (even though they are larger) because reading text requires familiarity (mostly) with lower-case letters. Phonics-based beginning reading should also be supplemented with phonemic awareness lessons, writing opportunities, and lots of read-alouds (literature read to children daily) to stimulate motivation, vocabulary development, concept development, and comprehension skill development. Many children with low vision can be successfully included in regular education environments. Parents may need to be vigilant to ensure that the school provides the teacher and students with appropriate low vision resources, for example technology in the classroom, classroom aide time, modified educational materials, and consultation assistance with low vision experts.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Communication">
        Communication
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Communication">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Communication with the visually impaired can be more difficult than communicating with someone who doesn't have vision loss. However, many people are uncomfortable with communicating with the blind, and this can cause communication barriers. One of the biggest obstacles in communicating with visually impaired individuals comes from face-to-face interactions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIALISTOCK_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BIALISTOCK-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are many factors that can cause the sighted to become uncomfortable while communicating face to face. There are many non-verbal factors that hinder communication between the visually impaired and the sighted, more often than verbal factors do. These factors, which Rivka Bialistock
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIALISTOCK_53-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-BIALISTOCK-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       mentions in her article, include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Lack of facial expressions, mimics, or body gestures/responses
       </li>
       <li>
        Non-verbal gestures that could imply the visually impaired individual not appearing interested
       </li>
       <li>
        Speaking when not anticipated or not speaking when anticipated
       </li>
       <li>
        Fear of offending the visually impaired
       </li>
       <li>
        Standing too close and invading the personal comfort level
       </li>
       <li>
        Having to exercise or ignore feelings of pity
       </li>
       <li>
        Being uncomfortable with touching objects or people
       </li>
       <li>
        A look of detachment or disengagement
       </li>
       <li>
        Dependency
       </li>
       <li>
        Being reminded of the fear of becoming blind
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The blind person sends these signals or types of non-verbal communication without being aware that they are doing so. These factors can all affect the way an individual would feel about communicating with the visually impaired. This leaves the visually impaired feeling rejected and lonely.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Adjusting_attitude">
        Adjusting attitude
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Adjusting attitude">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In the article
       <i>
        Towards better communication, from the interest point of view. Or—skills of sight-glish for the blind and visually impaired
       </i>
       , the author, Rivka Bialistock
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIALISTOCK_53-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-BIALISTOCK-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       comes up with a method to reduce individuals being uncomfortable with communicating with the visually impaired. This method is called blind-glish or sight-glish, which is a language for the blind, similar to English. For example, babies, who are not born and able to talk right away, communicate through sight-glish, simply seeing everything and communicating non-verbally. This comes naturally to sighted babies, and by teaching this same method to babies with a visual impairment can improve their ability to communicate better, from the very beginning.
      </p>
      <p>
       To avoid the rejected feeling of the visually impaired, people need to treat the blind the same way they would treat anyone else, rather than treating them like they have a disability, and need special attention. People may feel that it is improper to, for example, tell their blind child to look at them when they are speaking. However, this contributes to the sight-glish method.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIALISTOCK_53-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-BIALISTOCK-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is important to disregard any mental fears or uncomfortable feelings people have while communicating (verbally and non-verbally) face-to-face.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Surroundings">
        Surroundings
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Surroundings">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Individuals with a visual disability not only have to find ways to communicate effectively with the people around them, but their environment as well. The blind or visually impaired rely largely on their other senses such as hearing, touch, and smell in order to understand their surroundings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMES_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-JAMES-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sound">
        Sound
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Sound">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Sound is one of the most important senses that the blind or visually impaired use in order to locate objects in their surroundings. A form of echolocation is used, similarly to that of a bat.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Echolocation from a person's perspective is when the person uses sound waves generated from speech or other forms of noise such as cane tapping, which reflect off of objects and bounce back at the person giving them a rough idea of where the object is. This does not mean they can depict details based on sound but rather where objects are in order to interact, or avoid them. Increases in atmospheric pressure and humidity increase a person's ability to use sound to their advantage as wind or any form of background noise impairs it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMES_54-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-JAMES-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Touch">
        Touch
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Touch">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Touch is also an important aspect of how blind or visually impaired people perceive the world. Touch gives immense amount of information in the person's immediate surrounding. Feeling anything with detail gives off information on shape, size, texture, temperature, and many other qualities. Touch also helps with communication; braille is a form of communication in which people use their fingers to feel elevated bumps on a surface and can understand what is meant to be interpreted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are some issues and limitations with touch as not all objects are accessible to feel, which makes it difficult to perceive the actual object. Another limiting factor is that the learning process of identifying objects with touch is much slower than identifying objects with sight. This is due to the fact the object needs to be approached and carefully felt until a rough idea can be constructed in the brain.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMES_54-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-JAMES-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Smell">
        Smell
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Smell">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Certain smells can be associated with specific areas and help a person with vision problems to remember a familiar area. This way there is a better chance of recognizing an areas layout in order to navigate themselves through. The same can be said for people as well. Some people have their own special odor that a person with a more trained sense of smell can pick up. A person with an impairment of their vision can use this to recognize people within their vicinity without them saying a word.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMES_54-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-JAMES-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Communication_development">
        Communication development
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Communication development">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Visual impairment can have profound effects on the development of infant and child communication. The language and social development of a child or infant can be very delayed by the inability to see the world around them.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Social_development">
        Social development
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Social development">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Social development includes interactions with the people surrounding the infant in the beginning of its life. To a child with vision, a smile from a parent is the first symbol of recognition and communication, and is almost an instant factor of communication. For a visually impaired infant, recognition of a parent's voice will be noticed at approximately two months old, but a smile will only be evoked through touch between parent and baby. This primary form of communication is greatly delayed for the child and will prevent other forms of communication from developing. Social interactions are more complicated because subtle visual cues are missing and facial expressions from others are lost.
      </p>
      <p>
       Due to delays in a child's communication development, they may appear to be disinterested in social activity with peers, non-communicative and un-education on how to communicate with other people. This may cause the child to be avoided by peers and consequently over protected by family members.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Language_development">
        Language development
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Language development">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       With sight, much of what is learned by a child is learned through imitation of others, where as a visually impaired child needs very planned instruction directed at the development of postponed imitation. A visually impaired infant may jabber and imitate words sooner than a sighted child, but may show delay when combining words to say themselves, the child may tend to initiate few questions and their use of adjectives is infrequent. Normally the child's sensory experiences are not readily coded into language and this may cause them to store phrases and sentences in their memory and repeat them out of context. The language of the blind child does not seem to mirror their developing knowledge of the world, but rather their knowledge of the language of others.
      </p>
      <p>
       A visually impaired child may also be hesitant to explore the world around them due to fear of the unknown and also may be discouraged from exploration by overprotective family members. Without concrete experiences, the child is not able to develop meaningful concepts or the language to describe or think about them.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Healthcare_access">
        Healthcare access
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Healthcare access">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Visual impairment has the ability to create consequences for health and well being. Visual impairment is increasing especially among older people. It is recognized that those individuals with visual impairment are likely to have limited access to information and healthcare facilities, and may not receive the best care possible because not all health care professionals are aware of specific needs related to vision.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        A prerequisite of effective health care could very well be having staff that are aware that people may have problems with vision.
       </li>
       <li>
        Communication and different ways of being able to communicate with visually impaired clients must be tailored to individual needs and available at all times.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_58-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-58">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">
        Epidemiology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">
        WHO
       </a>
       estimates that in 2012 there were 285 million visually impaired people in the world, of which 246 million had low vision and 39 million were blind.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Of those who are blind 90% live in the developing world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_58-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Worldwide for each blind person, an average of 3.4 people have low vision, with country and regional variation ranging from 2.4 to 5.5.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-WHO-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        By age:
       </b>
       Visual impairment is unequally distributed across age groups. More than 82% of all people who are blind are 50 years of age and older, although they represent only 19% of the world's population. Due to the expected number of years lived in blindness (blind years), childhood blindness remains a significant problem, with an estimated 1.4 million blind children below age 15.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        By gender:
       </b>
       Available studies consistently indicate that in every region of the world, and at all ages, females have a significantly higher risk of being visually impaired than males.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        By geography:
       </b>
       Visual impairment is not distributed uniformly throughout the world. More than 90% of the world's visually impaired live in developing countries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_59-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-WHO-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Since the estimates of the 1990s, new data based on the 2002 global population show a reduction in the number of people who are blind or visually impaired, and those who are blind from the effects of infectious diseases, but an increase in the number of people who are blind from conditions related to longer life spans.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_59-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-WHO-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1987, it was estimated that 598,000 people in the United States met the legal definition of blindness.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirchner_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kirchner-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Of this number, 58% were over the age of 65.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirchner_60-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kirchner-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1994–1995, 1.3 million Americans reported legal blindness.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFB_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AFB-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">
        Society and culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Society and culture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_blind_people" title="List of blind people">
        List of blind people
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Blind_musicians" title="Blind musicians">
        Blind musicians
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_definition">
        Legal definition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Legal definition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       To determine which people qualify for special assistance because of their visual disabilities, various governments have specific definitions for legal blindness.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssdiqualify.org_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ssdiqualify.org-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">
        North America
       </a>
       and most of
       <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
        Europe
       </a>
       , legal blindness is defined as
       <a href="/wiki/Visual_acuity" title="Visual acuity">
        visual acuity
       </a>
       (vision) of 20/200 (6/60) or less in the better eye with best correction possible. This means that a legally blind individual would have to stand 20 feet (6.1 m) from an object to see it—with
       <a href="/wiki/Corrective_lens" title="Corrective lens">
        corrective lenses
       </a>
       —with the same degree of clarity as a normally sighted person could from 200 feet (61 m). In many areas, people with average acuity who nonetheless have a
       <a href="/wiki/Visual_field" title="Visual field">
        visual field
       </a>
       of less than 20
       <a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">
        degrees
       </a>
       (the norm being 180 degrees) are also classified as being legally blind. Approximately fifteen percent of those deemed legally blind, by any measure, have no light or form perception. The rest have some vision, from light perception alone to relatively good acuity.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_vision" title="Low vision">
        Low vision
       </a>
       is sometimes used to describe visual acuities from 20/70 to 20/200.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_art">
        Literature and art
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Literature and art">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Blindness_in_literature" title="Blindness in literature">
        Blindness in literature
       </a>
      </div>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">
        Antiquity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Antiquity">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moche_(culture)" title="Moche (culture)">
        Moche
       </a>
       people of ancient
       <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">
        Peru
       </a>
       depicted the blind in their ceramics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In Greek myth,
       <a href="/wiki/Tiresias" title="Tiresias">
        Tiresias
       </a>
       was a prophet famous for his
       <a href="/wiki/Clairvoyance" title="Clairvoyance">
        clairvoyance
       </a>
       . According to one myth, he was blinded by the gods as punishment for revealing their secrets, while another holds that he was blinded as punishment after he saw Athena naked while she was bathing. In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Odyssey" title="The Odyssey">
        the Odyssey
       </a>
       , the one-eyed Cyclops
       <a href="/wiki/Polyphemus" title="Polyphemus">
        Polyphemus
       </a>
       captures
       <a href="/wiki/Odysseus" title="Odysseus">
        Odysseus
       </a>
       , who blinds Polyphemus to escape. In Norse mythology,
       <a href="/wiki/Loki" title="Loki">
        Loki
       </a>
       tricks the blind god
       <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B6%C3%B0r" title="Höðr">
        Höðr
       </a>
       into killing his brother
       <a href="/wiki/Baldr" title="Baldr">
        Baldr
       </a>
       , the god of happiness.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
        New Testament
       </a>
       contains numerous instances of
       <a href="/wiki/Miracles_of_Jesus" title="Miracles of Jesus">
        Jesus performing miracles
       </a>
       to heal the blind. According to the Gospels, Jesus healed
       <a href="/wiki/Healing_the_two_blind_men_in_Galilee" title="Healing the two blind men in Galilee">
        the two blind men of Galilee
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Blind_man_of_Bethsaida" title="Blind man of Bethsaida">
        the blind man of Bethsaida
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Healing_the_blind_near_Jericho" title="Healing the blind near Jericho">
        the blind man of Jericho
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Healing_the_blind_at_birth" title="Healing the blind at birth">
        the man who was born blind
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The parable of the
       <a href="/wiki/Blind_men_and_an_elephant" title="Blind men and an elephant">
        blind men and an elephant
       </a>
       has crossed between many religious traditions and is part of
       <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">
        Jain
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">
        Buddhist
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">
        Sufi
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">
        Hindu
       </a>
       lore. In various versions of the tale, a group of blind men (or men in the dark) touch an
       <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">
        elephant
       </a>
       to learn what it is like. Each one feels a different part, but only one part, such as the side or the tusk. They then compare notes and learn that they are in complete disagreement.
      </p>
      <p>
       "
       <a href="/wiki/Three_Blind_Mice" title="Three Blind Mice">
        Three Blind Mice
       </a>
       " is a medieval
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Nursery_rhyme" title="Nursery rhyme">
        nursery rhyme
       </a>
       about three blind mice whose tails are cut off after chasing the farmer's wife. The work is explicitly incongruous, ending with the comment
       <i>
        Did you ever see such a sight in your life, As three blind mice?
       </i>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times">
        Modern times
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Modern times">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vel%C3%A1zquez_Blind_woman.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="434" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Vel%C3%A1zquez_Blind_woman.jpg/170px-Vel%C3%A1zquez_Blind_woman.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Vel%C3%A1zquez_Blind_woman.jpg/255px-Vel%C3%A1zquez_Blind_woman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Vel%C3%A1zquez_Blind_woman.jpg/340px-Vel%C3%A1zquez_Blind_woman.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vel%C3%A1zquez_Blind_woman.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Blind Woman
         </i>
         by
         <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Velázquez">
          Diego Velázquez
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jos%C3%A9_de_Ribera_018.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2704" data-file-width="2056" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Jos%C3%A9_de_Ribera_018.jpg/170px-Jos%C3%A9_de_Ribera_018.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Jos%C3%A9_de_Ribera_018.jpg/255px-Jos%C3%A9_de_Ribera_018.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Jos%C3%A9_de_Ribera_018.jpg/340px-Jos%C3%A9_de_Ribera_018.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jos%C3%A9_de_Ribera_018.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          The Sense of Touch
         </i>
         by
         <a href="/wiki/Jusepe_de_Ribera" title="Jusepe de Ribera">
          Jusepe de Ribera
         </a>
         depicts a blind man holding a marble head in his hands.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Poet
       <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">
        John Milton
       </a>
       , who went blind in mid-life, composed
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_His_Blindness" title="On His Blindness">
        On His Blindness
       </a>
       , a sonnet about coping with blindness. The work posits that
       <i>
        [those] who best Bear [God]'s mild yoke, they serve him best.
       </i>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Dutch painter and engraver
       <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">
        Rembrandt
       </a>
       often depicted scenes from the apocryphal
       <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Tobit" title="Book of Tobit">
        Book of Tobit
       </a>
       , which tells the story of a blind patriarch who is healed by his son, Tobias, with the help of the archangel
       <a href="/wiki/Raphael_(archangel)" title="Raphael (archangel)">
        Raphael
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Slaver-turned-abolitionist
       <a href="/wiki/John_Newton" title="John Newton">
        John Newton
       </a>
       composed the hymn
       <a href="/wiki/Amazing_Grace" title="Amazing Grace">
        Amazing Grace
       </a>
       about a wretch who
       <i>
        "once was lost, but now am found, Was blind, but now I see."
       </i>
       Blindness, in this sense, is used both metaphorically (to refer to someone who was ignorant but later became knowledgeable) and literally, as a reference to those healed in the Bible. In the later years of his life, Newton himself would go blind.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">
        H. G. Wells
       </a>
       ' story "
       <a href="/wiki/The_Country_of_the_Blind" title="The Country of the Blind">
        The Country of the Blind
       </a>
       " explores what would happen if a sighted man found himself trapped in a country of blind people to emphasise society's attitude to blind people by turning the situation on its head.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">
        Bob Dylan
       </a>
       's anti-war song "
       <a href="/wiki/Blowin%27_in_the_Wind" title="Blowin' in the Wind">
        Blowin' in the Wind
       </a>
       " twice alludes to metaphorical blindness:
       <i>
        How many times can a man turn his head // and pretend that he just doesn't see... How many times must a man look up // Before he can see the sky?
       </i>
      </p>
      <p>
       Contemporary fiction contains numerous well-known
       <a href="/wiki/Category:Fictional_blind_characters" title="Category:Fictional blind characters">
        blind characters
       </a>
       . Some of these characters can "see" by means of fictitious devices, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">
        Marvel Comics
       </a>
       superhero
       <a href="/wiki/Daredevil_(Marvel_Comics_character)" title="Daredevil (Marvel Comics character)">
        Daredevil
       </a>
       , who can "see" via his super-human hearing acuity, or
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Star_Trek" title="Star Trek">
         Star Trek
        </a>
        '
       </i>
       s
       <a href="/wiki/Geordi_La_Forge" title="Geordi La Forge">
        Geordi La Forge
       </a>
       , who can see with the aid of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VISOR" title="VISOR">
        VISOR
       </a>
       , a fictitious device that transmits optical signals to his brain.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">
        Sports
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Sports">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Blind and partially sighted people participate in sports, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Swimming_(sport)" title="Swimming (sport)">
        swimming
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snow_skiing" title="Snow skiing">
        snow skiing
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athletics_(sport)" title="Athletics (sport)">
        athletics
       </a>
       . Some sports have been invented or adapted for the blind, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Goalball" title="Goalball">
        goalball
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_association_football" title="Paralympic association football">
        association football
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Blind_cricket" title="Blind cricket">
        cricket
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blind_golf" title="Blind golf">
        golf
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Tennis" title="Tennis">
        tennis
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vicsports_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Vicsports-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The worldwide authority on sports for the blind is the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Blind_Sports_Federation" title="International Blind Sports Federation">
        International Blind Sports Federation
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Para_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Para-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tennis_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tennis-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       People with vision impairments have participated in the
       <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_Games" title="Paralympic Games">
        Paralympic Games
       </a>
       since the
       <a href="/wiki/1976_Summer_Paralympics" title="1976 Summer Paralympics">
        1976 Toronto summer Paralympics
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disserv_69-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-disserv-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphorical_uses">
        Metaphorical uses
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Metaphorical uses">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The word "blind" (adjective and verb) is often used to signify a lack of knowledge of something. For example, a blind date is a date in which the people involved have not previously met; a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blind_experiment" title="Blind experiment">
        blind experiment
       </a>
       is one in which information is kept from either the experimenter or the participant to mitigate the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placebo_effect" title="Placebo effect">
        placebo effect
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Observer_bias" title="Observer bias">
        observer bias
       </a>
       . The expression "blind leading the blind" refers to incapable people leading other incapable people. Being blind to something means not understanding or being aware of it. A "
       <a href="/wiki/Blind_spot_(vision)" title="Blind spot (vision)">
        blind spot
       </a>
       " is an area where someone cannot see: for example, where a car driver cannot see because parts of his car's bodywork are in the way; metaphorically, a topic on which an individual is unaware of their own biases, and therefore of the resulting distortions of their own judgements (see
       <a href="/wiki/Bias_blind_spot" title="Bias blind spot">
        Bias blind spot
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Research">
        Research
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Research">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Visual_prosthesis" title="Visual prosthesis">
        Visual prosthesis
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       A 2008 study tested the effect of using
       <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">
        gene therapy
       </a>
       to help restore the sight of patients with a rare form of inherited blindness, known as
       <a href="/wiki/Leber%27s_congenital_amaurosis" title="Leber's congenital amaurosis">
        Leber's congenital amaurosis
       </a>
       or LCA.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Leber's Congenital Amaurosis damages the light receptors in the retina and usually begins affecting sight in early childhood, with worsening vision until complete blindness around the age of 30.
      </p>
      <p>
       The study used a common cold virus to deliver a normal version of the gene called
       <a href="/wiki/RPE65" title="RPE65">
        RPE65
       </a>
       directly into the eyes of affected patients. Remarkably, all 3 patients, aged 19, 22 and 25, responded well to the treatment and reported improved vision following the procedure. Due to the age of the patients and the degenerative nature of LCA, the improvement of vision in gene therapy patients is encouraging for researchers. It is hoped that gene therapy may be even more effective in younger LCA patients who have experienced limited vision loss, as well as in other blind or partially blind individuals.
      </p>
      <p>
       Two experimental treatments for retinal problems include a cybernetic replacement and transplant of fetal retinal cells.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">
        Other animals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Visual_impairment&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Other animals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blind_animals" title="Blind animals">
        Blind animals
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Statements that certain species of
       <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">
        mammals
       </a>
       are "born blind" refers to them being born with their eyes closed and their eyelids fused together; the eyes open later. One example is the
       <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">
        rabbit
       </a>
       . In humans, the eyelids are fused for a while before birth, but open again before the normal birth time; however, very
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premature_babies" title="Premature babies">
        premature babies
       </a>
       are sometimes born with their eyes fused shut, and opening later. Other animals, such as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blind_mole_rat" title="Blind mole rat">
        blind mole rat
       </a>
       , are truly blind and rely on other senses.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The theme of blind animals has been a powerful one in literature.
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Shaffer" title="Peter Shaffer">
        Peter Shaffer
       </a>
       's Tony Award-winning play,
       <a href="/wiki/Equus_(play)" title="Equus (play)">
        <i>
         Equus
        </i>
       </a>
       , tells the story of a boy who blinds six horses.
       <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Taylor_(author)" title="Theodore Taylor (author)">
        Theodore Taylor
       </a>
       's classic young adult novel,
       <i>
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Trouble_With_Tuck&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Trouble With Tuck (page does not exist)">
         The Trouble With Tuck
        </a>
       </i>
       , is about a teenage girl, Helen, who trains her blind dog to follow and trust a seeing-eye dog.
      </p>
